<li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Christianity" title="Outline of Christianity">Outline</a></li></ul>
<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="18" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Reformation</b> (alternatively named the <b>Protestant Reformation</b> or the <b>European Reformation</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> was a major movement within <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a> in 16th-century Europe that posed a religious and political challenge to the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and in particular to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papal" title="Papal">papal</a> authority, arising from what were perceived to be <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Criticism of the Catholic Church">errors, abuses, and discrepancies</a> by the Catholic Church. The Reformation was the start of <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> and the split of the Western Church into Protestantism and what is now the Roman Catholic Church. It is also considered to be one of the events that signified the end of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies291_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies291-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, there were many <a href="/wiki/Proto-Protestantism" title="Proto-Protestantism">earlier reform movements</a>. Although the Reformation is usually considered to have started with the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i> by Martin Luther in 1517, he was not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excommunicated" title="Excommunicated">excommunicated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a> until January 1521. The <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a> of May 1521 condemned Luther and officially banned citizens of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> from defending or propagating his ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ENC3_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-ENC3-3">[3]</a></sup> The spread of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Gutenberg's</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> provided the means for the rapid dissemination of religious materials in the vernacular. Luther survived after being declared an outlaw due to the protection of Elector <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="Frederick III, Elector of Saxony">Frederick the Wise</a>. The initial movement in Germany diversified, and other reformers such as <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> arose. In general, the Reformers argued that <a href="/wiki/Salvation_in_Christianity" title="Salvation in Christianity">salvation in Christianity</a> was a completed status <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">based on faith in Jesus alone</a> and not a process that requires <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a>, as in the Catholic view. Key events of the period include: <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a> (1521), formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Prussia" title="Duchy of Prussia">Duchy of Prussia</a> (1525), <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> (1529 onwards), the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> (1545–63), the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Augsburg" title="Peace of Augsburg">Peace of Augsburg</a> (1555), the <a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">excommunication of Elizabeth I</a> (1570), <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Nantes" title="Edict of Nantes">Edict of Nantes</a> (1598) and <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> (1648). The <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>, also called the <i>Catholic Reformation</i> or the <i>Catholic Revival</i>, was the period of Catholic reforms initiated in response to the Protestant Reformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The end of the Reformation era is disputed.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Movements had been made towards a Reformation prior to Martin Luther, so some Protestants, such as <a href="/wiki/Landmarkism" title="Landmarkism">Landmark Baptists</a>, and the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a> prefer to credit the start of the Reformation to reformers such as <a href="/wiki/Arnold_of_Brescia" title="Arnold of Brescia">Arnold of Brescia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Waldo" title="Peter Waldo">Peter Waldo</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petr_Chel%C4%8Dick%C3%BD" title="Petr Chelčický">Petr Chelčický</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Savonarola" title="Girolamo Savonarola">Girolamo Savonarola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[a]</a></sup> Due to the reform efforts of Hus and other <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Reformation" title="Bohemian Reformation">Bohemian reformers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utraquist" title="Utraquist">Utraquist</a> <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussitism</a> was <a href="/wiki/Compacts_of_Basel" title="Compacts of Basel">acknowledged</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Basel" title="Council of Basel">Council of Basel</a> and was <a href="/wiki/Religious_peace_of_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora" title="Religious peace of Kutná Hora">officially tolerated</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_of_Bohemia" title="Crown of Bohemia">Crown of Bohemia</a>, although other movements were still subject to persecution, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lollards" title="Lollards">Lollards</a> in England and the <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a> in France and Italian regions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Luther began by criticising the sale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indulgences" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a>, insisting that the Pope had no authority over <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a> and that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasury_of_Merit" title="Treasury of Merit">Treasury of Merit</a> had no foundation in the Bible. The Reformation developed further to include a distinction between <a href="/wiki/Law_and_Gospel" title="Law and Gospel">Law and Gospel</a>, a complete reliance on Scripture as the only source of proper doctrine (<i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>) and the belief that <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">faith</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus</a> is the only way to receive God's pardon for sin (<i><a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">sola fide</a></i>) rather than good works. Although this is generally considered a Protestant belief, a similar formulation was taught by <a href="/wiki/Molinism" title="Molinism">Molinist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jansenism" title="Jansenism">Jansenist</a> Catholics. The <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_of_all_believers" title="Priesthood of all believers">priesthood of all believers</a> downplayed the need for saints or priests to serve as mediators, and mandatory <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">clerical celibacy</a> was ended. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simul_justus_et_peccator" title="Simul justus et peccator">Simul justus et peccator</a></i> implied that although people could improve, no one could become good enough to earn forgiveness from God. Sacramental theology was simplified and attempts at imposing Aristotelian epistemology were resisted.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Luther and his followers did not see these theological developments as changes. The 1530 <i><a href="/wiki/Augsburg_Confession" title="Augsburg Confession">Augsburg Confession</a></i> concluded that "in doctrine and ceremonies nothing has been received on our part against Scripture or the Church Catholic", and even after the <i>Council of Trent</i>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Chemnitz" title="Martin Chemnitz">Martin Chemnitz</a> published the 1565–73 <i><a href="/wiki/Examination_of_the_Council_of_Trent" title="Examination of the Council of Trent">Examination of the Council of Trent</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> as an attempt to prove that Trent innovated on doctrine while the Lutherans were following in the footsteps of the Church Fathers and Apostles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The initial movement in Germany diversified, and other reformers arose independently of Luther such as <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Theology of Huldrych Zwingli">Zwingli</a> in <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a> and John <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_John_Calvin" title="Theology of John Calvin">Calvin</a> in Geneva. Depending on the country, the Reformation had varying causes and different backgrounds and also unfolded differently than in Germany. The spread of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Gutenberg" title="Johannes Gutenberg">Gutenberg's</a> <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> provided the means for the rapid dissemination of religious materials in the vernacular.
</p><p>During Reformation-era <a href="/wiki/Confessionalization" title="Confessionalization">confessionalization</a>, Western Christianity adopted different confessions (<a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed_tradition" title="Reformed tradition">Reformed</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a>, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Radical Reformers, besides forming communities outside <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state sanction</a>, sometimes employed more extreme doctrinal change, such as the rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">tenets</a> of the councils of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicea" title="First Council of Nicea">Nicaea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Chalcedon</a> with the Unitarians of <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> movements were especially persecuted following the <a href="/wiki/German_Peasants%27_War" title="German Peasants' War">German Peasants' War</a>.
</p><p>Leaders within the Roman Catholic Church responded with the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a>, initiated by the <i><a href="/wiki/Confutatio_Augustana" title="Confutatio Augustana">Confutatio Augustana</a></i> in 1530, the <i><a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a></i> in 1545, the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuits</a> in 1540, the <a href="/wiki/Defensio_Tridentin%C3%A6_fidei" title="Defensio Tridentinæ fidei">Defensio Tridentinæ fidei</a> in 1578, and also a series of wars and expulsions of Protestants that continued until the 19th century. Northern Europe, with the exception of most of Ireland, came under the influence of Protestantism. Southern Europe remained predominantly Catholic apart from the much-persecuted <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a>. Central Europe was the site of much of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> and there were continued expulsions of Protestants in Central Europe up to the 19th century. Following World War II, the removal of ethnic Germans to either East Germany or Siberia reduced Protestantism in the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> countries, although some remain today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The absence of Protestants, however, does not necessarily imply a failure of the Reformation. Although Protestants were excommunicated and ended up worshipping in communions separate from Catholics (contrary to the original intention of the Reformers), they were also suppressed and persecuted in most of Europe at one point. As a result, some of them lived as <a href="/wiki/Crypto-Protestantism" title="Crypto-Protestantism">crypto-Protestants</a>, also called <a href="/wiki/Nicodemite" title="Nicodemite">Nicodemites</a>, contrary to the urging of John Calvin, who wanted them to live their faith openly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Crypto-Protestantism" title="Crypto-Protestantism">crypto-Protestants</a> have been identified as late as the 19th century after immigrating to Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martínez_Fernández_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martínez_Fernández-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_history">Origins and early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origins and early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Protestantism" title="History of Protestantism">History of Protestantism</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earlier_reform_movements">Earlier reform movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Earlier reform movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Protestantism" title="Proto-Protestantism">Proto-Protestantism</a></div>

class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Execution of <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Konstanz" title="Konstanz">Konstanz</a> (1415). <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western Christianity</a> was already formally compromised in the <a href="/wiki/Lands_of_the_Bohemian_Crown" title="Lands of the Bohemian Crown">Lands of the Bohemian Crown</a> long before Luther with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basel_Compacts" title="Basel Compacts">Basel Compacts</a> (1436) and the <a href="/wiki/Religious_peace_of_Kutn%C3%A1_Hora" title="Religious peace of Kutná Hora">Religious peace of Kutná Hora</a> (1485). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utraquist" title="Utraquist">Utraquist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussitism" title="Hussitism">Hussitism</a> was allowed there alongside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> confession. By the time the Reformation arrived, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Margraviate_of_Moravia" title="Margraviate of Moravia">Margraviate of Moravia</a> both had majority <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussite" title="Hussite">Hussite</a> populations for decades now.</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a> questioned the privileged status of the clergy which had bolstered their powerful role in England and the luxury and pomp of local parishes and their ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> He was accordingly characterised as the "evening star" of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a> and as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eosphoros" title="Eosphoros">morning star</a> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stella#Latin" title="wikt:stella">stella</a></i></span> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/matutina#Latin" title="wikt:matutina">matutina</a></i></span> of the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> In 1374, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Siena" title="Catherine of Siena">Catherine of Siena</a> began travelling with her followers throughout northern and central Italy advocating reform of the clergy and advising people that repentance and renewal could be done through "the total love for God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHollisterBennett2002342_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHollisterBennett2002342-14">[13]</a></sup> She carried on a long correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XI" title="Pope Gregory XI">Pope Gregory XI</a>, asking him to reform the clergy and the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>. The oldest Protestant churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a>, date their origins to <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> (John Huss) in the early 15th century. As it was led by a Bohemian noble majority, and recognised, for some time, by the Basel Compacts, the Hussite Reformation was Europe's first "<a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a>" because the ruling magistrates supported it, unlike the "<a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a>", which the state did not support.
</p><p>Common factors that played a role during the Reformation and the Counter-Reformation included the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a>, the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Cardinal-nephew" title="Cardinal-nephew">Cardinal-nephews</a>, and other corruption of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Curia" title="Roman Curia">Roman Curia</a> and other ecclesiastical hierarchy, the impact of <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>, the new learning of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism#High_Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a> that eroded loyalty to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>. Unrest due to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Great Schism of Western Christianity</a> (1378–1416) excited wars between princes, uprisings among the peasants, and widespread concern over corruption in the Church, especially from <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_University_in_Prague" title="Charles University in Prague">Charles University in Prague</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Hus objected to some of the practices of the Roman Catholic Church and wanted to return the church in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a> to earlier practices: <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">liturgy</a> in the language of the people (i.e. Czech), having lay people receive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communion_rite" title="Communion rite">communion</a> in both kinds (bread <i>and</i> wine—that is, in Latin, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utraquist" title="Utraquist">communio sub utraque specie</a>), married priests, and eliminating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indulgences" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a> and the concept of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a>. Some of these, like the use of local language as the liturgical language, were approved by the pope as early as in the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahoney2011_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahoney2011-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The leaders of the Roman Catholic Church condemned him at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> (1414–1417) and he was burnt at the stake, despite a promise of safe-conduct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ObWall54_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ObWall54-16">[15]</a></sup> Wycliffe was posthumously condemned as a heretic and his corpse exhumed and burned in 1428.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The Council of Constance confirmed and strengthened the traditional medieval conception of church and empire. The council did not address the national tensions or the theological tensions stirred up during the previous century and could not prevent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hussite_Wars" title="Hussite Wars">Hussite Wars</a> in Bohemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Surely we can source to something better than the 1911 Enc. Brit.? (February 2014)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_IV" title="Pope Sixtus IV">Pope Sixtus IV</a> (1471–1484) established the practice of selling indulgences to be applied to the dead, thereby establishing a new stream of revenue with agents across Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patrick1231_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patrick1231-19">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> (1492–1503) was one of the most controversial of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> popes. He was the father of seven children, including <a href="/wiki/Lucrezia_Borgia" title="Lucrezia Borgia">Lucrezia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Borgia" title="Cesare Borgia">Cesare Borgia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="BBC isn't high quality for historical purposes (March 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In response to papal corruption, particularly the sale of indulgences, Luther wrote <i>The Ninety-Five Theses</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Eyewitness to History series is not exactly high quality (March 2015)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A number of theologians in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> preached reformation ideas in the 1510s, shortly before or simultaneously with Luther, including <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Schappeler" title="Christoph Schappeler">Christoph Schappeler</a> in <a href="/wiki/Memmingen" title="Memmingen">Memmingen</a> (as early as 1513).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magisterial_Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Magisterial Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lutheranism#The_start_of_the_Reformation" title="History of Lutheranism">History of Lutheranism § The start of the Reformation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:334px;max-width:334px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:188px;max-width:188px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/186px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/279px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg/372px-Martin_Luther_by_Cranach-restoration.jpg 2x" width="186"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_95_Thesen.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="4611" data-file-width="3228" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/140px-Luther_95_Thesen.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/210px-Luther_95_Thesen.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Luther_95_Thesen.png/280px-Luther_95_Thesen.png 2x" width="140"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Martin Luther posted the <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i> in 1517</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/290px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/435px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/580px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Martin Luther at the <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a>, where he refused to recant his works when asked to by Charles V. (painting from <a href="/wiki/Anton_von_Werner" title="Anton von Werner">Anton von Werner</a>, 1877, <a href="/wiki/Staatsgalerie_Stuttgart" title="Staatsgalerie Stuttgart">Staatsgalerie Stuttgart</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The Reformation is usually dated to 31 October 1517 in <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a>, Saxony, when Luther sent his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ninety-Five_Theses" title="The Ninety-Five Theses">Ninety-Five Theses on the Power and Efficacy of Indulgences</a></i> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Mainz" title="Archbishop of Mainz">Archbishop of Mainz</a>. The theses <a href="/wiki/Debate" title="Debate">debated</a> and criticised the Church and the papacy, but concentrated upon the selling of indulgences and doctrinal policies about <a href="/wiki/Purgatory#Protestantism" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment#Reformation_concepts" title="Particular judgment">particular judgment</a>, and the authority of the pope. He would later in the period 1517–1521 write works on devotion to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, the intercession of and devotion to the saints, the sacraments, mandatory clerical celibacy, and later on the authority of the pope, the ecclesiastical law, censure and excommunication, the role of secular rulers in religious matters, the relationship between Christianity and the law, <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a>, and monasticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schofield122_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield122-22">[21]</a></sup> Some nuns, such as <a href="/wiki/Katharina_von_Bora" title="Katharina von Bora">Katharina von Bora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ursula_of_Munsterberg" title="Ursula of Munsterberg">Ursula of Munsterberg</a>, left the monastic life when they accepted the Reformation, but other orders adopted the Reformation, as Lutherans continue to have <a href="/wiki/Template:Lutheran_orders" title="Template:Lutheran orders">monasteries today</a>. In contrast, Reformed areas typically secularised monastic property.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Reformers and their opponents made heavy use of inexpensive pamphlets as well as vernacular Bibles using the relatively new printing press, so there was swift movement of both ideas and documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin270_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtkinsonFitzgerald15_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-AtkinsonFitzgerald15-24">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Magdalena_Heymair" title="Magdalena Heymair">Magdalena Heymair</a> printed pedagogical writings for teaching children Bible stories.
</p><p>Parallel to events in Germany, a movement began in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Switzerland" title="History of Switzerland">Switzerland</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a>. These two movements quickly agreed on most issues, but some unresolved differences kept them separate. Some followers of Zwingli believed that the Reformation was too conservative, and moved independently toward more radical positions, some of which survive among modern day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>.
</p><p>After this first stage of the Reformation, following the <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> of Luther in <i><a href="/wiki/Decet_Romanum_Pontificem" title="Decet Romanum Pontificem">Decet Romanum Pontificem</a></i> and the condemnation of his followers by the edicts of the 1521 Diet of Worms, the work and writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> were influential in establishing a loose consensus among various churches in Switzerland, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Scotland" title="History of Scotland">Scotland</a>, Hungary, Germany and elsewhere.
</p><p>Although the <a href="/wiki/German_Peasants%27_War" title="German Peasants' War">German Peasants' War</a> of 1524–1525 began as a tax and anti-corruption protest as reflected in the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Articles" title="Twelve Articles">Twelve Articles</a>, its leader <a href="/wiki/Thomas_M%C3%BCntzer" title="Thomas Müntzer">Thomas Müntzer</a> gave it a radical Reformation character. It swept through the Bavarian, <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabian</a> principalities, including the <a href="/wiki/Black_Company" title="Black Company">Black Company</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florian_Geier" title="Florian Geier">Florian Geier</a>, a knight from <a href="/wiki/Giebelstadt" title="Giebelstadt">Giebelstadt</a> who joined the peasants in the general outrage against the Catholic hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> In response to reports about the destruction and violence, Luther condemned the revolt in writings such as <i><a href="/wiki/Against_the_Murderous,_Thieving_Hordes_of_Peasants" title="Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants">Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants</a></i>; Zwingli and Luther's ally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a> also did not condone the uprising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Some 100,000 peasants were killed by the end of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radical_Reformation">Radical Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Radical Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a></div>
<p>The Radical Reformation was the response to what was believed to be the corruption in both the Roman Catholic Church and the <a href="/wiki/Magisterial_Reformation" title="Magisterial Reformation">Magisterial Reformation</a>. Beginning in Germany and Switzerland in the 16th century, the Radical Reformation developed radical Protestant churches throughout Europe. The term includes <a href="/wiki/Thomas_M%C3%BCntzer" title="Thomas Müntzer">Thomas Müntzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zwickau_prophets" title="Zwickau prophets">Zwickau prophets</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptists</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Hutterites" title="Hutterites">Hutterites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mennonites" title="Mennonites">Mennonites</a>.
</p><p>In parts of Germany, Switzerland, and Austria, a majority sympathised with the Radical Reformation despite intense persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-horsch_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-horsch-29">[28]</a></sup> Although the surviving proportion of the European population that rebelled against Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwinglian" title="Zwinglian">Zwinglian</a> churches was small, Radical Reformers wrote profusely and the literature on the Radical Reformation is disproportionately large, partly as a result of the proliferation of the Radical Reformation teachings in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite significant diversity among the early Radical Reformers, some "repeating patterns" emerged among many Anabaptist groups. Many of these patterns were enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Schleitheim_Confession" title="Schleitheim Confession"><i>Schleitheim Confession</i> (1527)</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Believer%27s_baptism" title="Believer's baptism">believers' (or adult) baptism</a>, memorial view of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Lord's Supper</a>, belief that Scripture is the final authority on matters of faith and practice, emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, interpretation of Scripture in community, separation from the world and a <a href="/wiki/Two_kingdoms_doctrine" title="Two kingdoms doctrine">two-kingdom theology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nonresistance" title="Nonresistance">nonresistance</a>, communal ownership and economic sharing, belief in the freedom of the will, non-swearing of oaths, "yieldedness" (<i>Gelassenheit</i>) to one's community and to God, the <a href="/wiki/Shunning" title="Shunning">ban</a> (i.e., shunning), salvation through divinization (<i>Vergöttung</i>) and ethical living, and discipleship (<i>Nachfolge Christi</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Literacy">Literacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Literacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/220px-Lutherbibel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/330px-Lutherbibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/440px-Lutherbibel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Martin Luther's 1534 Bible translated into German</a>. Luther's translation influenced the development of the current Standard German.</div></div></div>
<p>The Reformation was a triumph of literacy and the new printing press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cameron_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cameron-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin270_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-35">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther's translation of the Bible</a> into German was a decisive moment in the spread of literacy, and stimulated as well the printing and distribution of religious books and pamphlets. From 1517 onward, religious pamphlets flooded Germany and much of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1530, over 10,000 publications are known, with a total of ten million copies. The Reformation was thus a media revolution. Luther strengthened his attacks on Rome by depicting a "good" against "bad" church. From there, it became clear that print could be used for propaganda in the Reformation for particular agendas, although the term propaganda derives from the Catholic <i><a href="/wiki/Congregation_for_the_Evangelization_of_Peoples" title="Congregation for the Evangelization of Peoples">Congregatio de Propaganda Fide</a></i> (<i>Congregation for Propagating the Faith</i>) from the Counter-Reformation. Reform writers used existing styles, cliches and stereotypes which they adapted as needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-36">[34]</a></sup> Especially effective were writings in German, including Luther's translation of the Bible, his <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism">Smaller Catechism</a> for parents teaching their children, and his <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Large_Catechism" title="Luther's Large Catechism">Larger Catechism</a>, for pastors.
</p><p>Using the German vernacular they expressed the Apostles' Creed in simpler, more personal, Trinitarian language. Illustrations in the German Bible and in many tracts popularised Luther's ideas. <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a> (1472–1553), the great painter patronised by the electors of Wittenberg, was a close friend of Luther, and he illustrated Luther's theology for a popular audience. He dramatised Luther's views on the relationship between the Old and New Testaments, while remaining mindful of Luther's careful distinctions about proper and improper uses of visual imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weimer387_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weimer387-39">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes_of_the_Reformation">Causes of the Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Causes of the Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2290" data-file-width="1618" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/170px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/255px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Holbein-erasmus.jpg/340px-Holbein-erasmus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Holbein-erasmus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a> was a Catholic priest who inspired some of the Protestant reformers</div></div></div>
<p>The following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supply-side" title="Supply-side">supply-side</a> factors have been identified as causes of the Reformation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The presence of a <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> in a city by 1500 made Protestant adoption by 1600 far more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin270_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-23">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li>Protestant literature was produced at greater levels in cities where media markets were more competitive, making these cities more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-35">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>Ottoman incursions decreased conflicts between Protestants and Catholics, helping the Reformation take root.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Greater political autonomy increased the likelihood that Protestantism would be adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin270_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>Where Protestant reformers enjoyed princely patronage, they were much more likely to succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim_188–215_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim_188–215-43">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Proximity to neighbours who adopted Protestantism increased the likelihood of adopting Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-42">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cities that had higher numbers of students enrolled in heterodox universities and lower numbers enrolled in orthodox universities were more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim_188–215_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kim_188–215-43">[40]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The following demand-side factors have been identified as causes of the Reformation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Cities with strong cults of saints were less likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cities where <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> was practised were less likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li>Regions that were poor but had great economic potential and bad political institutions were more likely to adopt Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>The presence of bishoprics made the adoption of Protestantism less likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin270_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin270-23">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li>The presence of monasteries made the adoption of Protestantism less likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46">[43]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>A 2020 study linked the spread of Protestantism to personal ties to Luther (e.g. letter correspondents, visits, former students) and trade routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_in_Germany">Reformation in Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Reformation in Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a></div>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="31" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/44px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/66px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/88px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="44"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2018</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:443px;max-width:443px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4605" data-file-width="5772" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg/300px-Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg/450px-Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg/600px-Deutschland_im_XVI._Jahrhundert.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Political situation in Germany about 1560</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_religions_1560.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1858" data-file-width="1062" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Europe_religions_1560.jpg/135px-Europe_religions_1560.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Europe_religions_1560.jpg/203px-Europe_religions_1560.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Europe_religions_1560.jpg/270px-Europe_religions_1560.jpg 2x" width="135"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Religious situation in Germany and Europe about 1560</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 1517, Luther nailed the <i>Ninety-five theses</i> to the Castle Church door, and without his knowledge or prior approval, they were copied and printed across Germany and internationally. Different reformers arose more or less independently of Luther in 1518 (for example <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Melanchthon" title="Philip Melanchthon">Philip Melanchthon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Schnepf" title="Erhard Schnepf">Erhard Schnepf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brenz" title="Johannes Brenz">Johannes Brenz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a>) and in 1519 (for example <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolaus_von_Amsdorf" title="Nikolaus von Amsdorf">Nikolaus von Amsdorf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Hutten" title="Ulrich von Hutten">Ulrich von Hutten</a>), and so on.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_Disputation" title="Heidelberg Disputation">Heidelberg Disputation</a> (1518) where Luther described the <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_the_Cross" title="Theology of the Cross">Theology of the Cross</a> as opposed to the Theology of Glory and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leipzig_Disputation" title="Leipzig Disputation">Leipzig Disputation</a> (1519), the faith issues were brought to the attention of other German theologians throughout the Empire. Each year drew new theologians to embrace the Reformation and participate in the ongoing, European-wide discussion about faith. The pace of the Reformation proved unstoppable by 1520.
</p><p>The early Reformation in Germany mostly concerns the life of Martin Luther until he was excommunicated by Pope Leo X on 3 January 1521, in the bull <i><a href="/wiki/Decet_Romanum_Pontificem" title="Decet Romanum Pontificem">Decet Romanum Pontificem</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> The exact moment <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> realised the key doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_Martin_Luther#Justification_by_Faith" title="Theology of Martin Luther">Justification by Faith</a> is described in German as the <i><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther#Justification_by_faith_alone" title="Martin Luther">Turmerlebnis</a></i>. In <i><a href="/wiki/Table_Talk_(Luther)" title="Table Talk (Luther)">Table Talk</a></i>, Luther describes it as a sudden realization. Experts often speak of a gradual process of realization between 1514 and 1518.
</p><p>Reformation ideas and Protestant church services were first introduced in cities, being supported by local citizens and also some nobles. The Reformation did not receive overt state support until 1525, although it was only due to the protection of Elector <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="Frederick III, Elector of Saxony">Frederick the Wise</a> (who had a strange dream<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> the night prior to 31 October 1517) that Luther survived after being declared an outlaw, in hiding <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther#At_Wartburg_Castle" title="Martin Luther">at Wartburg Castle</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther#Return_to_Wittenberg_and_Peasants'_War" title="Martin Luther">returning to Wittenberg</a>. It was more of a movement among the German people between 1517 and 1525, and then also a political one beginning in 1525. Reformer <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Clarenbach" title="Adolf Clarenbach">Adolf Clarenbach</a> was burned at the stake near Cologne in 1529.
</p><p>The first state to formally adopt a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> confession was the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Prussia" title="Duchy of Prussia">Duchy of Prussia</a> (1525). <a href="/wiki/Albert,_Duke_of_Prussia" title="Albert, Duke of Prussia">Albert, Duke of Prussia</a> formally declared the "Evangelical" faith to be the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a>. Catholics <a href="/wiki/Labelling#Labelling_in_argumentation" title="Labelling">labeled</a> self-identified Evangelicals "Lutherans" to discredit them after the practice of naming a heresy after its founder. However, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a> traditionally sees itself as the "main trunk of the historical Christian Tree" founded by Christ and the Apostles, holding that during the Reformation, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Church of Rome</a> fell away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Remensnyder1893_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Remensnyder1893-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frey1918_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frey1918-51">[48]</a></sup> Ducal Prussia was followed by many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_free_cities" title="Imperial free cities">imperial free cities</a> and other minor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="States of the Holy Roman Empire">imperial entities</a>. The next sizable territories were the <a href="/wiki/Landgraviate_of_Hesse" title="Landgraviate of Hesse">Landgraviate of Hesse</a> (1526; at the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Homberg" title="Synod of Homberg">Synod of Homberg</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a> (1527; Luther's homeland), <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Palatinate" title="Electoral Palatinate">Electoral Palatinate</a> (1530s), and the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Duchy of Württemberg">Duchy of Württemberg</a> (1534). For a more complete list, see the <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_by_the_date_of_adoption_of_the_Reformation" title="List of states by the date of adoption of the Reformation">list of states by the date of adoption of the Reformation</a> and the table of the <a href="/wiki/Augsburg_Confession#Years_of_adoption" title="Augsburg Confession">adoption years for the Augsburg Confession</a>. The reformation wave swept first the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire#Reformation_and_Renaissance" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, and then extended beyond it to the rest of the European continent.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Germany was home to the greatest number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_reformers" title="Protestant reformers">Protestant reformers</a>. Each state which turned Protestant had their own reformers who contributed towards the <i>Evangelical</i> faith. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electoral_Saxony" title="Electoral Saxony">Electoral Saxony</a> the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical-Lutheran_Church_of_Saxony" title="Evangelical-Lutheran Church of Saxony">Evangelical-Lutheran Church of Saxony</a> was organised and served as an example for other states, although Luther was not dogmatic on questions of polity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_outside_Germany">Reformation outside Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reformation outside Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2017</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Reformation also spread widely throughout Europe, starting with Bohemia, in the Czech lands, and, over the next few decades, to other countries.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria">Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Austria#Austria_in_the_Reformation_and_Counter-Reformation_(1517–1564)" title="History of Austria">History of Austria § Austria in the Reformation and Counter-Reformation (1517–1564)</a></div>
<p>Austria followed the same pattern as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German-speaking" title="German-speaking">German-speaking</a> states within the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, and Lutheranism became the main Protestant confession among its population. <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a significant following in the eastern half of present-day Austria, while <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> was less successful. Eventually the expulsions of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation#politics" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> reversed the trend.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Czech_lands">Czech lands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Czech lands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Reformation" title="Bohemian Reformation">Bohemian Reformation</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a> were a Christian movement in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> following the teachings of Czech reformer <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Jan_Hus">Jan Hus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Jan Hus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a></div>
<p>Czech reformer and university professor <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a> (c. 1369–1415) became the best-known representative of the Bohemian Reformation and one of the forerunners of the Protestant Reformation.
</p><p>Jan Hus was declared a heretic and executed—burned at stake—at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> in 1415 where he arrived voluntarily to defend his teachings.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Hussite_movement">Hussite movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hussite movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tranoscius.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/170px-Tranoscius.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/255px-Tranoscius.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Tranoscius.JPG/340px-Tranoscius.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tranoscius.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ji%C5%99%C3%AD_T%C5%99anovsk%C3%BD" title="Jiří Třanovský">Jiří Třanovský</a> (1592–1637), the "<a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a> of the Slavs" who was active in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> (Upper Hungary)</div></div></div>
<p>This predominantly religious movement was propelled by social issues and strengthened Czech national awareness. In 1417, two years after the execution of Jan Hus, the Czech reformation quickly became the chief force in the country.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hussites" title="Hussites">Hussites</a> made up the vast majority of the population, forcing the Council of Basel to recognize in 1437 a system of two "religions" for the first time, signing the <a href="/wiki/Compacts_of_Basel" title="Compacts of Basel">Compacts of Basel</a> for the kingdom (Catholic and Czech <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraquists" title="Ultraquists">Ultraquism</a> a Hussite movement). Bohemia later also elected two Protestant kings (<a href="/wiki/George_of_Pod%C4%9Bbrady" title="George of Poděbrady">George of Poděbrady</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_V_of_the_Palatinate" title="Frederick V of the Palatinate">Frederick of Palatine</a>).
</p><p>After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburgs" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a> took control of the region, the Hussite churches were prohibited and the kingdom partially recatholicised. Even later, <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> gained a substantial following, after being permitted by the Habsburgs with the continued persecution of the Czech native Hussite churches. Many Hussites thus declared themselves Lutherans.
</p><p>Two churches with Hussite roots are now the second and third biggest churches among the largely agnostic peoples: <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Church_of_Czech_Brethren" title="Evangelical Church of Czech Brethren">Czech Brethren</a> (which gave origin to the international church known as the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Hussite_Church" title="Czechoslovak Hussite Church">Czechoslovak Hussite Church</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Switzerland" title="Reformation in Switzerland">Reformation in Switzerland</a></div>
<p>In Switzerland, the teachings of the reformers and especially those of Zwingli and Calvin had a profound effect, despite frequent quarrels between the different branches of the Reformation.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Huldrych_Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Huldrych Zwingli">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Z%C3%BCrich" title="Reformation in Zürich">Reformation in Zürich</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/170px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/255px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg/340px-Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ulrich-Zwingli-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a> launched the Reformation in Switzerland. Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Asper" title="Hans Asper">Hans Asper</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Parallel to events in Germany, a movement began in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swiss_Confederation" title="Swiss Confederation">Swiss Confederation</a> under the leadership of Huldrych Zwingli. Zwingli was a scholar and preacher who moved to <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Zürich">Zürich</a>—the then-leading city state—in 1518, a year after Martin Luther began the Reformation in Germany with his <a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a>. Although the two movements agreed on many issues of theology, as the recently introduced <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> spread ideas rapidly from place to place, some unresolved differences kept them separate. Long-standing resentment between the German states and the Swiss Confederation led to heated debate over how much Zwingli owed his ideas to Lutheranism. Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwinglianism" title="Zwinglianism">Zwinglianism</a> does hold uncanny resemblance to Lutheranism (it even had its own equivalent of the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i>, called the 67 Conclusions), historians have been unable to prove that Zwingli had any contact with Luther's publications before 1520, and Zwingli himself maintained that he had prevented himself from reading them.
</p><p>The German Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_of_Hesse" title="Philip of Hesse">Philip of Hesse</a> saw potential in creating an alliance between Zwingli and Luther, seeing strength in a united Protestant front. A meeting was held in his castle in 1529, now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloquy_of_Marburg" title="Colloquy of Marburg">Colloquy of Marburg</a>, which has become infamous for its complete failure. The two men could not come to any agreement due to their disputation over one key doctrine. Although Luther preached <a href="/wiki/Consubstantiation" title="Consubstantiation">consubstantiation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> over <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">transubstantiation</a>, he believed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_presence_of_Christ" title="Real presence of Christ">real presence of Christ</a> in the Communion bread. Zwingli, inspired by Dutch theologian <a href="/wiki/Honius" title="Honius">Cornelius Hoen</a>, believed that the Communion bread was only representative and memorial—Christ was not present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Luther became so angry that he famously carved into the meeting table in chalk <i>Hoc Est Corpus Meum</i>—a Biblical quotation from the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a> meaning "This is my body". Zwingli countered this saying that <i>est</i> in that context was the equivalent of the word <i>significat</i> (signifies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some followers of Zwingli believed that the Reformation was too conservative and moved independently toward more radical positions, some of which survive among modern day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptists</a>. One famous incident illustrating this was when radical Zwinglians fried and ate sausages during Lent in Zurich city square by way of protest against the Church teaching of <a href="/wiki/Good_works" title="Good works">good works</a>. Other Protestant movements grew up along the lines of mysticism or humanism (cf. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Erasmus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Berquin" title="Louis de Berquin">Louis de Berquin</a> who was martyred in 1529), sometimes breaking from Rome or from the Protestants, or forming outside of the churches.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Calvin">John Calvin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: John Calvin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Geneva" title="Republic of Geneva">Republic of Geneva</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1174" data-file-width="955" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/170px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/255px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg/340px-John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Calvin_-_Young.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> was one of the leading figures of the Reformation. His legacy remains in a variety of churches.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> of Luther and condemnation of the Reformation by the Pope, the work and writings of John Calvin were influential in establishing a loose consensus among various churches in Switzerland, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, Hungary, Germany and elsewhere. After the expulsion of its Bishop in 1526, and the unsuccessful attempts of the Berne reformer <a href="/wiki/William_Farel" title="William Farel">Guillaume (William) Farel</a>, Calvin was asked to use the organisational skill he had gathered as a student of law to discipline the "fallen city" of Geneva. His "Ordinances" of 1541 involved a collaboration of Church affairs with the City council and <a href="/wiki/Consistory_(Protestantism)#Reformed_usage" title="Consistory (Protestantism)">consistory</a> to bring morality to all areas of life. After the establishment of the Geneva academy in 1559, Geneva became the unofficial capital of the Protestant movement, providing refuge for Protestant exiles from all over Europe and educating them as Calvinist missionaries. These missionaries dispersed Calvinism widely, and formed the French <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a> in Calvin's own lifetime and spread to Scotland under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> in 1560. <a href="/wiki/Anne_Locke" title="Anne Locke">Anne Locke</a> translated some of Calvin's writings to English around this time. The faith continued to spread after Calvin's death in 1563 and reached as far as Constantinople by the start of the 17th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Reformation foundations engaged with <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinianism</a>. Both Luther and Calvin thought along lines linked with the theological teachings of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>. The Augustinianism of the Reformers struggled against <a href="/wiki/Pelagianism" title="Pelagianism">Pelagianism</a>, a heresy that they perceived in the Catholic Church of their day. Ultimately, since Calvin and Luther disagreed strongly on certain matters of theology (such as double-predestination and Holy Communion), the relationship between Lutherans and Calvinists was one of conflict.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nordic_countries">Nordic countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nordic countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
