<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Denmark-Norway_and_Holstein" title="Reformation in Denmark-Norway and Holstein">Reformation in Denmark-Norway and Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Iceland#The_Reformation" title="Religion in Iceland">Religion in Iceland § The Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Norway#From_Reformation_to_1964" title="Religion in Norway">Religion in Norway § From Reformation to 1964</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Sweden#Lutheran_Reformation" title="Religion in Sweden">Religion in Sweden § Lutheran Reformation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henrik_sormi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henrik_sormi.JPG/220px-Henrik_sormi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henrik_sormi.JPG/330px-Henrik_sormi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Henrik_sormi.JPG/440px-Henrik_sormi.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henrik_sormi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The seal of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishopric_of_Turku" title="Bishopric of Turku">Diocese of Turku</a> (Finland) during the 16th and 17th centuries featured the finger of St Henry. The post-Reformation diocese included the relic of a pre-Reformation saint in its seal.</div></div></div>
<p>All of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> ultimately adopted Lutheranism over the course of the 16th century, as the monarchs of Denmark (who also ruled Norway and Iceland) and Sweden (who also ruled Finland) converted to that faith.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sweden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Sweden" title="Reformation in Sweden">Reformation in Sweden</a></div>
<p>In Sweden, the Reformation was spearheaded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gustav_Vasa" title="Gustav Vasa">Gustav Vasa</a>, elected king in 1523, with major contributions by <a href="/wiki/Olaus_Petri" title="Olaus Petri">Olaus Petri</a>, a Swedish clergyman. Friction with the pope over the latter's interference in Swedish ecclesiastical affairs led to the discontinuance of any official connection between Sweden and the papacy since 1523. Four years later, at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diet_of_V%C3%A4ster%C3%A5s" title="Diet of Västerås">Diet of Västerås</a>, the king succeeded in forcing the diet to accept his dominion over the national church. The king was given possession of all church property, church appointments required royal approval, the clergy were subject to the civil law, and the "pure Word of God" was to be preached in the churches and taught in the schools—effectively granting official sanction to Lutheran ideas. The <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession#Lutheran_churches" title="Apostolic succession">apostolic succession</a> was retained in Sweden during the Reformation. The adoption of Lutheranism was also one of the main reasons for the eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Dacke_War" title="Dacke War">Dacke War</a>, a peasants uprising in Småland.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland#Part_of_the_Church_of_Sweden" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland § Part of the Church of Sweden</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I_of_Denmark" title="Frederick I of Denmark">Frederick I</a> (1523–33), Denmark remained officially Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockhart200761_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockhart200761-54">[51]</a></sup> Frederick initially pledged to persecute Lutherans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockhart200760_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockhart200760-55">[52]</a></sup> yet he quickly adopted a policy of protecting Lutheran preachers and reformers, of whom the most famous was <a href="/wiki/Hans_Tausen" title="Hans Tausen">Hans Tausen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockhart200761_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockhart200761-54">[51]</a></sup> During his reign, Lutheranism made significant inroads among the Danish population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockhart200761_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockhart200761-54">[51]</a></sup> In 1526, Frederick forbade papal investiture of bishops in Denmark and in 1527 ordered fees from new bishops be paid to the crown, making Frederick the head of the church of Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockhart200761_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockhart200761-54">[51]</a></sup> Frederick's son, Christian, was openly Lutheran, which prevented his election to the throne upon his father's death. In 1536, following his victory in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count%27s_War" title="Count's War">Count's War</a>, he became king as <a href="/wiki/Christian_III_of_Denmark" title="Christian III of Denmark">Christian III</a> and continued the <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Denmark%E2%80%93Norway_and_Holstein" title="Reformation in Denmark–Norway and Holstein">Reformation of the state church</a> with assistance from <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">Johannes Bugenhagen</a>. By the Copenhagen recess of October 1536, the authority of the Catholic bishops was terminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerntson200664_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerntson200664-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Faroe_Islands">Faroe Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Faroe Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Faroe_Islands#Reformation_era" title="History of the Faroe Islands">History of the Faroe Islands § Reformation era</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Iceland">Iceland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Iceland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_Reformation" title="Icelandic Reformation">Icelandic Reformation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a>'s influence had already reached <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> before King Christian's decree. The <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">Germans</a> fished near Iceland's coast, and the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> engaged in commerce with the Icelanders. These Germans raised a Lutheran church in <a href="/wiki/Hafnarfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Hafnarfjörður">Hafnarfjörður</a> as early as 1533. Through German trade connections, many young <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">Icelanders</a> studied in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> In 1538, when the kingly decree of the new Church ordinance reached Iceland, bishop <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%96gmundur&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ögmundur (page does not exist)">Ögmundur</a> and his clergy denounced it, threatening excommunication for anyone subscribing to the German "heresy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jón_R._Hjálmarsson_1993_p._70_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jón_R._Hjálmarsson_1993_p._70-58">[55]</a></sup> In 1539, the King sent a new governor to Iceland, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Klaus_von_Mervitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Klaus von Mervitz (page does not exist)">Klaus von Mervitz</a>, with a mandate to introduce reform and take possession of church property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jón_R._Hjálmarsson_1993_p._70_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jón_R._Hjálmarsson_1993_p._70-58">[55]</a></sup> Von Mervitz seized a monastery in <a href="/wiki/Vi%C3%B0ey" title="Viðey">Viðey</a> with the help of his sheriff, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dietrich_of_Minden&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dietrich of Minden (page does not exist)">Dietrich of Minden</a>, and his soldiers. They drove the monks out and seized all their possessions, for which they were promptly excommunicated by Ögmundur.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Protestant_martyrs_of_the_English_Reformation" title="List of Protestant martyrs of the English Reformation">List of Protestant martyrs of the English Reformation</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_of_England">Church of England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Church of England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3589" data-file-width="2041" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:After_Hans_Holbein_the_Younger_-_Portrait_of_Henry_VIII_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> broke England's ties with the Roman Catholic Church, becoming the sole head of the English Church.</div></div></div>
<p>The separation of the Church of England from Rome under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, beginning in 1529 and completed in 1537, brought England alongside this broad Reformation movement. Although <a href="/wiki/Robert_Barnes_(martyr)" title="Robert Barnes (martyr)">Robert Barnes</a> attempted to get Henry VIII to adopt Lutheran theology, he refused to do so in 1538 and burned him at the stake in 1540. Reformers in the Church of England alternated, for decades, between sympathies between Catholic tradition and Reformed principles, gradually developing, within the context of robustly Protestant doctrine, a tradition considered a middle way (<i><a href="/wiki/Via_media" title="Via media">via media</a></i>) between the Catholic and Protestant traditions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The English Reformation followed a different course from the Reformation in continental Europe. There had long been a strong strain of <a href="/wiki/Anti-clericalism" title="Anti-clericalism">anti-clericalism</a>. England had already given rise to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lollard" title="Lollard">Lollard</a> movement of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, which played an important part in inspiring the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussite" title="Hussite">Hussites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>. Lollardy was suppressed and became an underground movement, so the extent of its influence in the 1520s is difficult to assess. The different character of the English Reformation came rather from the fact that it was driven initially by the political necessities of Henry VIII.
</p><p>Henry had once been a sincere Catholic and had even authored a book strongly criticising Luther. His wife, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, bore him only a single child who survived infancy, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a>. Henry strongly wanted a male heir, and many of his subjects might have agreed, if only because they wanted to avoid another dynastic conflict like the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3129" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/170px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/255px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg/340px-Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Cranmer_by_Gerlach_Flicke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a> proved essential in the development of the English Reformation.</div></div></div>
<p>Refused an annulment of his marriage to Catherine, King Henry decided to remove the Church of England from the authority of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> In 1534, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy" title="Act of Supremacy">Act of Supremacy</a> recognised Henry as "the only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Head" title="Supreme Head">Supreme Head</a> on earth of the Church of England".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bray113_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-bray113-60">[57]</a></sup> Between 1535 and 1540, under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell,_1st_Earl_of_Essex" title="Thomas Cromwell, 1st Earl of Essex">Thomas Cromwell</a>, the policy known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> was put into effect. The veneration of some <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saints</a>, certain pilgrimages and some pilgrim shrines were also attacked. Huge amounts of church land and property passed into the hands of the Crown and ultimately into those of the nobility and gentry. The vested interest thus created made for a powerful force in support of the dissolution.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>There were some notable opponents to the Henrician Reformation, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_More" title="Thomas More">Thomas More</a> and Cardinal <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher" title="John Fisher">John Fisher</a>, who were executed for their opposition. There was also a growing party of reformers who were imbued with the Calvinistic, Lutheran and Zwinglian doctrines then current on the Continent. When Henry died he was succeeded by his Protestant son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward VI</a>, who, through his empowered councillors (with the King being only nine years old at his succession and fifteen at his death) the Duke of Somerset and the Duke of Northumberland, ordered the destruction of images in churches, and the closing of the <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">chantries</a>. Under Edward VI the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> moved closer to continental Protestantism.
</p><p>Yet, at a popular level, religion in England was still in a state of flux. Following a brief Catholic restoration during the reign of Mary (1553–1558), a loose consensus developed during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a>, though this point is one of considerable debate among historians. This "<a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>" largely formed <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> into a distinctive church tradition. The compromise was uneasy and was capable of veering between extreme <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> on one hand and Catholicism on the other. But compared to the bloody and chaotic state of affairs in contemporary France, it was relatively successful, in part because Queen Elizabeth lived so long, until the Puritan Revolution or <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> in the seventeenth century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="English_dissenters">English dissenters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: English dissenters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritanism" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">English Dissenters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_(religion)" title="Independent (religion)">Independent (religion)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">Nonconformism</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Presbyterianism" title="English Presbyterianism">English Presbyterianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_separatism" title="Ecclesiastical separatism">Ecclesiastical separatism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/17th-century_denominations_in_England" title="17th-century denominations in England">17th-century denominations in England</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2916" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/220px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/330px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/440px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> was a devout Puritan and military leader, who became Lord Protector of England, Scotland, and Ireland.</div></div></div>
<p>The success of the <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> on the Continent and the growth of a Puritan party dedicated to further Protestant reform polarised the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Age" title="Elizabethan Age">Elizabethan Age</a>, although it was not until the 1640s that England underwent religious strife comparable to what its neighbours had suffered some generations before.
</p><p>The early <i>Puritan movement</i> (late 16th–17th centuries) was Reformed (or <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a>) and was a movement for reform in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. Its origins lay in the discontent with the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>. The desire was for the Church of England to resemble more closely the Protestant churches of Europe, especially <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>. The Puritans objected to ornaments and ritual in the churches as <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatrous</a> (vestments, surplices, organs, genuflection), calling the vestments "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papist" title="Papist">popish</a> pomp and rags" (see <a href="/wiki/Vestments_controversy" title="Vestments controversy">Vestments controversy</a>). They also objected to ecclesiastical courts. Their refusal to endorse completely all of the ritual directions and formulas of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i>, and the imposition of its liturgical order by legal force and inspection, sharpened Puritanism into a definite opposition movement.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The later Puritan movement, often referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissenters" title="Dissenters">dissenters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist_(Protestantism)" title="Nonconformist (Protestantism)">nonconformists</a>, eventually led to the formation of various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed_tradition" title="Reformed tradition">Reformed</a> <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denominations</a>.
</p><p>The most famous emigration to America was the migration of Puritan separatists from the Anglican Church of England. They fled first to Holland, and then later to America to establish the English <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">colony of Massachusetts</a> in New England, which later became one of the original United States. These Puritan separatists were also known as "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilgrim_Fathers" title="Pilgrim Fathers">Pilgrims</a>". After establishing a colony at <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Colony" title="Plymouth Colony">Plymouth</a> (which became part of the colony of Massachusetts) in 1620, the Puritan pilgrims received a charter from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_England" title="King of England">King of England</a> that legitimised their colony, allowing them to do trade and commerce with merchants in England, in accordance with the principles of <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a>. The Puritans persecuted those of other religious faiths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> for example, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Hutchinson" title="Anne Hutchinson">Anne Hutchinson</a> was banished to Rhode Island during the <a href="/wiki/Antinomian_Controversy" title="Antinomian Controversy">Antinomian Controversy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_Dyer" title="Mary Dyer">Mary Dyer</a> was hanged in Boston for repeatedly defying a Puritan law banning Quakers from the colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PER_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-PER-62">[59]</a></sup> She was one of the four executed Quakers known as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_martyrs" title="Boston martyrs">Boston martyrs</a>. Executions ceased in 1661 when <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King Charles II</a> explicitly forbade Massachusetts from executing anyone for professing Quakerism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHLS_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHLS-63">[60]</a></sup> In 1647, Massachusetts passed a law prohibiting any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit Roman Catholic</a> priests from entering territory under Puritan jurisdiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> Any suspected person who could not clear himself was to be banished from the colony; a second offence carried a death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Pilgrims held radical Protestant disapproval of Christmas, and its celebration was outlawed in Boston from 1659 to 1681.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-66">[63]</a></sup> The ban was revoked in 1681 by the English-appointed governor <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Andros" title="Edmund Andros">Edmund Andros</a>, who also revoked a Puritan ban on festivities on Saturday nights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-66">[63]</a></sup> Nevertheless, it was not until the mid-19th century that celebrating Christmas became fashionable in the Boston region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Wales#Early_modern_period" title="History of Wales">History of Wales § Early modern period</a></div>
<p>Bishop <a href="/wiki/Richard_Davies_(bishop)" title="Richard Davies (bishop)">Richard Davies</a> and dissident Protestant cleric <a href="/wiki/John_Penry" title="John Penry">John Penry</a> introduced Calvinist theology to Wales. In 1588, the Bishop of Llandaff published the entire Bible in the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>. The translation had a significant impact upon the Welsh population and helped to firmly establish Protestantism among the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> The Welsh Protestants used the model of the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Dort" title="Synod of Dort">Synod of Dort</a> of 1618–1619. Calvinism developed through the Puritan period, following the restoration of the monarchy under Charles II, and within Wales' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinistic_Methodist" title="Calvinistic Methodist">Calvinistic Methodist</a> movement. However few copies of Calvin's writings were available before mid-19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Scottish Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_preaching.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1837" data-file-width="1896" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/220px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/330px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/John_Knox_preaching.JPG/440px-John_Knox_preaching.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_preaching.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> was a leading figure in the Scottish Reformation</div></div></div>
<p>The Reformation in Scotland's case culminated ecclesiastically in the establishment of a church along <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed_theology" title="Reformed theology">reformed</a> lines, and politically in the triumph of English influence over that of France. <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> is regarded as the leader of the Scottish reformation.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation_Parliament" title="Scottish Reformation Parliament">Reformation Parliament</a> of 1560 repudiated the pope's authority by the <i><a href="/wiki/Papal_Jurisdiction_Act_1560" title="Papal Jurisdiction Act 1560">Papal Jurisdiction Act 1560</a></i>, forbade the celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a> and approved a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confession_of_Faith" title="Confession of Faith">Confession of Faith</a>. It was made possible by a revolution against French hegemony under the regime of the <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Guise" title="Mary of Guise">Mary of Guise</a>, who had governed Scotland in the name of her absent daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a> (then also <a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">Queen</a> of France).
</p><p>Although Protestantism triumphed relatively easily in Scotland, the exact form of Protestantism remained to be determined. The 17th century saw a complex struggle between <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterianism</a> (particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Episcopalianism</a>. The Presbyterians eventually won control of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, which went on to have an important influence on Presbyterian churches worldwide, but Scotland retained a relatively large <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Episcopalian minority</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Estonia">Estonia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Estonia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Estonia#The_Reformation" title="History of Estonia">History of Estonia § The Reformation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massacre_of_M%C3%A9rindol" title="Massacre of Mérindol">Massacre of Mérindol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_of_France" title="Reformed Church of France">Reformed Church of France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">French Wars of Religion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Richelieu,_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_(detalle).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="849" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/220px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/330px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg/440px-Richelieu%2C_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_%28detalle%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Richelieu,_por_Philippe_de_Champaigne_(detalle).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Although a Catholic clergyman himself, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a> allied France with Protestant states.</div></div></div>
<p>Besides the Waldensians already present in France, Protestantism also spread in from German lands, where the Protestants were nicknamed <i><a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a></i>; this eventually led to decades of civil warfare.
</p><p>Though not personally interested in religious reform, <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> (reigned 1515–1547) initially maintained an attitude of tolerance, in accordance with his interest in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Humanism" title="Renaissance Humanism">humanist</a> movement. This changed in 1534 with the <a href="/wiki/Affair_of_the_Placards" title="Affair of the Placards">Affair of the Placards</a>. In this act, Protestants denounced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Mass" title="Catholic Mass">Catholic Mass</a> in placards that appeared across France, even reaching the royal apartments. During this time as the issue of religious faith entered into the arena of politics, Francis came to view the movement as a threat to the kingdom's stability.
</p><p>Following the Affair of the Placards, culprits were rounded up, at least a dozen heretics were put to death, and the persecution of Protestants increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> One of those who fled France at that time was John Calvin, who emigrated to Basel in 1535 before eventually settling in Geneva in 1536. Beyond the reach of the French kings in Geneva, Calvin continued to take an interest in the religious affairs of his native land including the training of ministers for congregations in France.
</p><p>As the number of Protestants in France increased, the number of heretics in prisons awaiting trial also grew. As an experimental approach to reduce the caseload in Normandy, a special court just for the trial of heretics was established in 1545 in the <a href="/wiki/Parlement_de_Normandie" title="Parlement de Normandie">Parlement de Rouen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II</a> took the throne in 1547, the persecution of Protestants grew and special courts for the trial of heretics were also established in the Parlement de Paris. These courts came to known as <a href="/wiki/Chambre_Ardente" title="Chambre Ardente">"<i>La Chambre Ardente</i>"</a> ("the fiery chamber") because of their reputation of meting out death penalties on burning gallows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite heavy persecution by Henry II, the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_of_France" title="Reformed Church of France">Reformed Church of France</a>, largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinist" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> in direction, made steady progress across large sections of the nation, in the urban <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>, appealing to people alienated by the obduracy and the complacency of the Catholic establishment.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9,_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/220px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/330px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg/440px-La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9%2C_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_masacre_de_San_Bartolom%C3%A9,_por_Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" title="Saint Bartholomew's Day massacre">Saint Bartholomew's Day massacre</a>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Dubois" title="François Dubois">François Dubois</a></div></div></div>
<p>French Protestantism, though its appeal increased under persecution, came to acquire a distinctly political character, made all the more obvious by the conversions of nobles during the 1550s. This established the preconditions for a series of destructive and intermittent conflicts, known as the <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">Wars of Religion</a>. The civil wars gained impetus with the sudden death of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_France" title="Henry II of France">Henry II</a> in 1559, which began a prolonged period of weakness for the French crown. <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/atrocity" title="wikt:atrocity">Atrocity</a> and outrage became the defining characteristics of the time, illustrated at their most intense in the <a href="/wiki/St._Bartholomew%27s_Day_massacre" title="St. Bartholomew's Day massacre">St. Bartholomew's Day massacre</a> of August 1572, when the Catholic party killed between 30,000 and 100,000 Huguenots across France. The wars only concluded when <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a>, himself a former Huguenot, issued the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Nantes" title="Edict of Nantes">Edict of Nantes</a> (1598), promising official toleration of the Protestant minority, but under highly restricted conditions. Catholicism remained the official state religion, and the fortunes of French Protestants gradually declined over the next century, culminating in Louis XIV's <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Fontainebleau" title="Edict of Fontainebleau">Edict of Fontainebleau</a> (1685), which revoked the Edict of Nantes and made Catholicism the sole legal religion of France, leading some Huguenots to live as <a href="/wiki/Nicodemite" title="Nicodemite">Nicodemites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> In response to the Edict of Fontainebleau, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_William_I,_Elector_of_Brandenburg" title="Frederick William I, Elector of Brandenburg">Frederick William I, Elector of Brandenburg</a> declared the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Potsdam" title="Edict of Potsdam">Edict of Potsdam</a> (October 1685), giving free passage to Huguenot refugees and tax-free status to them for ten years.
</p><p>In the late 17th century, 150,000–200,000 Huguenots fled to England, the Netherlands, Prussia, Switzerland, and the English and Dutch overseas colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> A significant community in France remained in the <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A9vennes" title="Cévennes">Cévennes</a> region. A separate Protestant community, of the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> faith, existed in the newly conquered province of <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a>, its status not affected by the Edict of Fontainebleau.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Spain#Phillip_II_and_the_wars_of_religion" title="History of Spain">History of Spain § Phillip II and the wars of religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Spain" title="Protestantism in Spain">Protestantism in Spain</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:130px;max-width:130px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:217px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1845" data-file-width="1085" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/128px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/192px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg/256px-Francisco_de_Enzinas-Nuevo_Testamento.001.jpg 2x" width="128"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The New Testament translated by Enzinas, published in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> (1543)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:158px;max-width:158px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:217px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leizarraga_biblia_01.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="295" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Leizarraga_biblia_01.png/156px-Leizarraga_biblia_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Leizarraga_biblia_01.png/234px-Leizarraga_biblia_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Leizarraga_biblia_01.png 2x" width="156"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The New Testament translated by <a href="/wiki/Joanes_Leizarraga" title="Joanes Leizarraga">Joanes Leizarraga</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque language</a> (1571) on the orders of Navarre's Calvinist queen, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeanne_III_of_Navarre" title="Jeanne III of Navarre">Jeanne III of Navarre</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In the early 16th century, Spain had a different political and cultural milieu from its Western and Central European neighbours in several respects, which affected the mentality and the reaction of the nation towards the Reformation. Spain, which had only recently managed to complete the reconquest of the Peninsula from the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> in 1492, had been preoccupied with converting the Muslim and Jewish populations of the newly conquered regions through the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Inquisition" title="Spanish Inquisition">Spanish Inquisition</a> in 1478. The rulers of the nation stressed political, cultural, and religious unity, and by the time of the Lutheran Reformation, the Spanish Inquisition was already 40 years old and had the capability of quickly persecuting any new movement that the leaders of the Catholic Church perceived or interpreted to be religious heterodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pettegree304_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pettegree304-77">[74]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Charles_V" title="Emperor Charles V">Charles V</a> did not wish to see Spain or the rest of Habsburg Europe divided, and in light of continual threat from the Ottomans, preferred to see the Roman Catholic Church reform itself from within. This led to a <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> in Spain in the 1530s. During the 1520s, the Spanish Inquisition had created an atmosphere of suspicion and sought to root out any religious thought seen as suspicious. As early as 1521, the Pope had written a letter to the Spanish monarchy warning against allowing the unrest in Northern Europe to be replicated in Spain. Between 1520 and 1550, printing presses in Spain were tightly controlled and any books of Protestant teaching were prohibited.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="765" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg/220px-Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg/330px-Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg/440px-Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Contemporary_illustration_of_the_Auto-da-fe_held_at_Validolid_Spain_21-05-1559..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary illustration of the <a href="/wiki/Auto-da-f%C3%A9" title="Auto-da-fé">auto-da-fé</a> of <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a>, in which fourteen Protestants were burned at the stake for their faith, on 21 May 1559</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1530 and 1540, Protestantism in Spain was still able to gain followers clandestinely, and in cities such as <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a> adherents would secretly meet at private houses to pray and study the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Estep299_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Estep299-78">[75]</a></sup> Protestants in Spain were estimated at between 1000 and 3000, mainly among intellectuals who had seen writings such as those of <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>. Notable reformers included Dr. Juan Gil and Juan Pérez de Pineda who subsequently fled and worked alongside others such as <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Enzinas" title="Francisco de Enzinas">Francisco de Enzinas</a> to translate the Greek <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> into the Spanish language, a task completed by 1556. Protestant teachings were smuggled into Spain by Spaniards such as Julián Hernández, who in 1557 was condemned by the Inquisition and burnt at the stake. Under <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a>, conservatives in the Spanish church tightened their grip, and those who refused to recant such as Rodrigo de Valer were condemned to life imprisonment. In May 1559, sixteen Spanish Lutherans were burnt at the stake: fourteen were strangled before being burnt, while two were burnt alive. In October another thirty were executed. Spanish Protestants who were able to flee the country were to be found in at least a dozen cities in Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, where some of them embraced <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> teachings. Those who fled to England were given support by the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Kingdom of Navarre</a>, although by the time of the Protestant Reformation a minor principality territoriality restricted to southern France, had French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> monarchs, including <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV of France</a> and his mother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeanne_III_of_Navarre" title="Jeanne III of Navarre">Jeanne III of Navarre</a>, a devout Calvinist.
</p><p>Upon the arrival of the Protestant Reformation, Calvinism reached some <a href="/wiki/Basques" title="Basques">Basques</a> through the translation of the Bible into the <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque language</a> by <a href="/wiki/Joanes_Leizarraga" title="Joanes Leizarraga">Joanes Leizarraga</a>. As Queen of Navarre, Jeanne III commissioned the translation of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> into Basque<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[d]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%A9arnese_language" title="Béarnese language">Béarnese</a> for the benefit of her subjects.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Molinism" title="Molinism">Molinism</a> presented a soteriology similar to Protestants within the Roman Catholic Church.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Portugal" title="Protestantism in Portugal">Protestantism in Portugal</a></div>
<p>During the Reformation era Protestantism was unsuccessful in Portugal, as its spread was frustrated for similar reasons to those in Spain.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion_in_the_Netherlands" title="History of religion in the Netherlands">History of religion in the Netherlands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5283" data-file-width="6594" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg/220px-Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg/330px-Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg/440px-Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dirk.willems.rescue.ncs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anabaptist <a href="/wiki/Dirk_Willems" title="Dirk Willems">Dirk Willems</a> rescues his pursuer and is subsequently burned at the stake in 1569.</div></div></div>
<p>The Reformation in the Netherlands, unlike in many other countries, was not initiated by the rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Seventeen_Provinces" title="Seventeen Provinces">Seventeen Provinces</a>, but instead by multiple popular movements which in turn were bolstered by the arrival of Protestant refugees from other parts of the continent. While the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> movement enjoyed popularity in the region in the early decades of the Reformation, Calvinism, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Dutch Reformed Church</a>, became the dominant Protestant faith in the country from the 1560s onward. In the early 17th century internal <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Dort" title="Synod of Dort">theological conflict</a> within the Calvinist church between two tendencies of Calvinism, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gomarists" title="Gomarists">Gomarists</a> and the liberal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arminians" title="Arminians">Arminians</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Remonstrants" title="Remonstrants">Remonstrants</a>), resulted in Gomarist Calvinism becoming the <i>de facto</i> state religion.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Belgium">Belgium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Belgium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The first two Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Category:Protestant_martyrs" title="Category:Protestant martyrs">martyrs</a> were monks from Antwerp, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Esch_and_Heinrich_Voes" title="Johann Esch and Heinrich Voes">Johann Esch and Heinrich Hoes</a>, who were burned at the stake when they would not recant.
</p><p>Harsh <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inquisition_of_the_Netherlands" title="Inquisition of the Netherlands">persecution</a> of Protestants by the Spanish government of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a> contributed to a desire for independence in the provinces, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Years%27_War" title="Eighty Years' War">Eighty Years' War</a> and, eventually, the separation of the largely Protestant <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> from the Catholic-dominated <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Southern Netherlands</a> (present-day Belgium).
</p><p>In 1566, at the peak of Belgian Reformation, there were an estimated 300,000 Protestants, or 20% of the Belgian population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia">Latvia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reformation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Latvia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Latvia#German_period,_1185–1561" title="History of Latvia">History of Latvia § German period, 1185–1561</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>.
